<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="post-partum-haemorrhage-pph">Post Partum Haemorrhage (PPH)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#post-partum-haemorrhage-pph" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>PPH can be defined as excessive bleeding of more than 500mls of blood from the genital tract after the birth of a baby or any amount that may lead to deterioration in the mother's condition. This is also known as Primary PPH. If the condition occurs after 24 hours of, and within six weeks after, delivery it is known as Secondary PPH.</p>
<p>There are several predisposing factors associated with PPH.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="can-you-think-what-theses-are">Can you think what theses are?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#can-you-think-what-theses-are" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Grand multipara, that is, high parity of more than five children and advanced age</li>
<li>Precipitate labour, that is, rapidly progressing labour</li>
<li>Over distension of the uterus resulting from polyhydramnios, multiple pregnancy or overly large infant</li>
<li>Previous history of PPH</li>
<li>Blood clotting/coagulation disorders and disseminated intravascular coagulation</li>
<li>Preeclampsia and eclampsia</li>
<li>Heavy sedative medication during labour or general anaesthesia</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="primary-post-partum-haemorrhage">Primary Post Partum Haemorrhage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#primary-post-partum-haemorrhage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Bleeding occurs in the third stage of labour and within 24 hours after delivery. The main sources of the haemorrhage include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Placental site</li>
<li>Lacerations of the genital tract</li>
<li>Blood coagulation disorders</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="haemorrhage-from-the-placental-site">Haemorrhage from the Placental Site<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#haemorrhage-from-the-placental-site" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Bleeding can also result from the mismanagement of the third stage of labour. If ergometrine or syntocinon is not given at the end of the second stage, it remains quiescent for a few minutes. While the placenta is still attached, no bleeding occurs. However, if there are any attempts to manipulate the uterus such as massaging, kneading, squeezing and pushing, this will over-stimulate the uterus and cause uncoordinated uterine contractions, hence bleeding will begin.</p>
<p>Injudicious attempts to expel the placenta before complete separation will cause PPH as well as inversion of the uterus. Additionally, a full/distended bladder during the third stage inhibits proper placental separation leading to haemorrhage.</p>
<p>An abnormally adherent placenta can also be problematic. In placenta acreta chorionic villi penetrate the myometrium to the peritoneal surface of the uterus. In placental praevia, the placenta has a wider area of attachment than normal and the lower uterine segment may fail to contract strongly enough to control bleeding.</p>
<p>Disseminated Intravascular Coagulation (DIC) and other clotting disorders are rare but can cause slow but persistent and dangerous haemorrhage. DIC is especially associated with concealed placenta, abrupt, amniotic embolism and dead foetus. Finally, fibroids can interfere with good muscular action and prevent the closure of the blood sinuses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-are-the-signs-of-possible-pph">What are the Signs of Possible PPH?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-are-the-signs-of-possible-pph" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>â˜ There is an escape of blood through the vagina.</p>
<p>â˜ There is an abnormally high fundus and the uterus feels larger and softer than normal.</p>
<p>â˜ The patient becomes shocked especially with concealed haemorrhage.</p>
<p>â˜ With excessive haemorrhage the blood pressure falls, the pulse rate rises, pallor and air hunger may occur.</p>
<p>â˜ Post partum necrosis of the anterior lobe of the pituitary gland is rare and occurs with low level blood pressure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention-of-primary-post-partum-haemorrhage">Prevention of Primary Post Partum Haemorrhage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-of-primary-post-partum-haemorrhage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You should be able to identify risk factors during the antenatal period through accurate history taking. Anaemia must be corrected during pregnancy. You should encourage hospital delivery, especially for women with a history of PPH, grand multipara or a history of APH.</p>
<p>For the mothers at risk of PPH infusion of syntocinon during labour until two hours after third stage is effective and safe for the mother. In case of prolonged labour induction can be done by use of syntocinon drip.</p>
<p>Ergomentrine or syntocinon should be given at crowning of the head or after delivery of the anterior shoulder of the foetus. The bladder should be emptied at the end of the first stage of labour. In anticipation of blood coagulation disorders, you should have ready two units of blood for women at risk.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 204 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-primary-postpartum-haemorrhage">Management of Primary Postpartum Haemorrhage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-primary-postpartum-haemorrhage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The main principles of treatment involve arresting the bleeding, restoration of the blood volume and treatment of circulatory failure (shock).</p>
<p>You should ascertain whether the uterus is contracted, if it is not, you should rub it to cause a contraction. Ask your assistant to repeat syntometrine if you administered this at the end of the second stage. If you had not, they can begin to administer syntometrine, put up an IV infusion of normal saline and call the doctor.</p>
<p>When you feel a contraction, deliver the placenta by using control cord transition. When the placenta is out you should continue to rub until the final contractions expel the clots and the bleeding stops.</p>
<p>However, if the mother is still bleeding and the placenta is out, the uterus is not well contracted. Quickly check for the completeness of the placenta. If the placenta is complete, perform a bimanual compression whereby the right hand is inserted into the vagina in the anterior fornix above the cervix, and the left hand is placed on the abdomen and pressed downwards onto the posterior wall of the uterus so that it is compressed between the two hands. The doctor will order syntocinon drip 40 units at 40 drops per minute.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="maintain-firm-pressure-till-the-uterus-is-felt-to-contract-bleeding-will-stop">Maintain firm pressure till the uterus is felt to contract; bleeding will stop.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#maintain-firm-pressure-till-the-uterus-is-felt-to-contract-bleeding-will-stop" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-primary-postpartum-haemorrhage">Management of Primary Postpartum Haemorrhage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-primary-postpartum-haemorrhage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If the placenta is not complete, do a bimanual compression IV with 40 units of syntocinon. Ask your assistant to inform theatre staff to prepare for exploration under general anaesthesia. Prepare the patient for theatre, ready to be transferred when the doctor arrives.</p>
<p>If the placenta has not separated and remains in the upper uterine segment, manual removal of the placenta under general anaesthesia is performed. If bleeding still continues (and is not due to a clotting defect) tying of the internal iliac arteries or even hysterectomy may be considered.</p>
<p>However, you should check the haemoglobin level and determine the need for haematinics or transfusion with blood. If the placenta is delivered and is complete and the uterus is well contracted but the mother is still bleeding, check the birth canal for any tear, which may be the cause of bleeding.</p>
<p>If the placenta is delivered, the uterus is well contracted and there is no tear of the perineum, which is bleeding, then you should suspect coagulation disorders. The doctor will commence the mother on fibrogen while preparation is made to transfuse fresh blood.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="haemorrhage-from-lacerations">Haemorrhage from Lacerations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#haemorrhage-from-lacerations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Bleeding resulting from lacerations may occur from the cervix, the wall of the vagina, at the apex of an episiotomy wound, and/or a tear.</p>
<p>This usually occurs during the Spontaneous Vaginal Delivery (SVD) of a large (macrosonic) baby or when a large diameter presents in face, extraction of the after coming head in breech presentation, or due to difficult instrumental delivery.</p>
<p>The main signs of SVD are that the bleeding starts immediately the baby is born, the blood flow is continuous and/or the uterus is firmly contracted.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="can-you-think-of-the-management-of-lacerations">Can you think of the management of lacerations?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#can-you-think-of-the-management-of-lacerations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-management-of-lacerations">The Management of Lacerations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-management-of-lacerations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In a lithotomy position examine the patient's birth canal to determine the location and extent of the tear. Apply pressure to the tears till they are sutured. Use aseptic technique to suture the tears. Remember that proper retractors and instruments are needed to suture a high cervical tear.</p>
<p>Profuse haemorrhage from a cervical tear involving a branch of the uterine artery can be temporarily controlled by clamping the artery with an artery forceps till the patient is taken to theatre. A seat bath after the suturing helps to clean the area. Antibiotics should be prescribed to prevent infection. You should also determine the level of bleeding to determine the need for transfusion or haematinics.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="blood-coagulation-disorders">Blood Coagulation Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#blood-coagulation-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In the event that the patient has a coagulation disorder, she may bleed profusely. You can diagnose this by doing a bedside clotting time to rule out DIC. Clotting time that is more than seven minutes is suggestive of DIC. In this case start management as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Keep the patient warm</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Give fresh whole blood</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Give fresh frozen plasma</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Give platelets concentrates</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>This type of care may not be available in small health institutions hence the need for referral. If bleeding persists utero-ovarian artery ligation or subtotal hysterectomy should be considered.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="secondary-post-partum-haemorrhage">Secondary Post Partum Haemorrhage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#secondary-post-partum-haemorrhage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This usually occurs 24 hours after delivery or up to six weeks post delivery. The mother may present with retention of a placenta piece/products of conception, blood clots or puerperal sepsis, especially due to caesarean section. There may be low grade fever, lochia is usually heavier than normal and bright red in colour. Sub involution is present.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="can-you-think-how-to-manage-a-secondary-post-haemorrhage">Can you think how to manage a secondary post haemorrhage?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#can-you-think-how-to-manage-a-secondary-post-haemorrhage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-management-of-a-secondary-post-haemorrhage">The Management of a Secondary Post Haemorrhage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-management-of-a-secondary-post-haemorrhage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Mothers are usually readmitted during the puerperal period. Call for the doctor. If the uterus still palpates, stimulate it to induce a contraction and expel blood clots. Give ergometrine 1ml. Keep all pads and linen to assess blood volume. The doctor may order an ultrasound to rule out any retained products of conception. The result will determine whether the mother will be prepared for exploration under general anaesthetic or treated conservatively with antibiotic and oral ergometrine.</p>
<p>Haemoglobin is estimated before discharge. If it is below 9g/dl, the option of iron replacement is discussed with the mother. The severity of anaemia determines the appropriate care with foods rich in iron or iron supplements. In severe cases the mother is transfused.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ruptured-uterus">Ruptured Uterus<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ruptured-uterus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a serious complication, which should not occur in today's obstetric care where there is good prenatal and intra partum care.</p>
<p>The rupture of the uterus is a tear in the wall of the uterus. This tear is divided into two categories.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complete-or-intra-peritoneal">Complete or Intra Peritoneal<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complete-or-intra-peritoneal" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a tear in the wall of the uterus, which involves the endometrium, myometrium and perimetrium/peritoneum.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="incomplete-or-extra-peritoneal">Incomplete or Extra Peritoneal<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#incomplete-or-extra-peritoneal" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the tearing of the uterus, which involves the endometrium and myometrium. Tears can occur parentally, during labour or delivery and may endanger the lives of both mother and foetus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="can-you-think-of-the-predisposing-factors-that-may-lead-to-ruptured-uterus">Can you think of the predisposing factors that may lead to ruptured uterus?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#can-you-think-of-the-predisposing-factors-that-may-lead-to-ruptured-uterus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-factors-that-may-lead-to-ruptured-uterus">The factors that may lead to ruptured uterus<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-factors-that-may-lead-to-ruptured-uterus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Predisposing factors include those that contribute to over distension of the uterus such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>Polyhydramnious and multiple pregnancy</li>
<li>Pregnancy occurring within six months post Caesarean section with the placenta situated on the scar</li>
<li>Obstetric manipulation or operations such as during internal cephalic version</li>
<li>Previous operation of the uterus</li>
<li>Foetal pelvic disproportion</li>
<li>Myomectomy</li>
</ul>
<p>Signs of ruptured uterus include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Rupture may be gradual with bleeding</li>
<li>Pain and tenderness at the central region are present when the rupture is incomplete</li>
<li>Diagnosis is difficult; therefore close monitoring is very important.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-causes-of-ruptured-uterus">The Causes of Ruptured Uterus<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-causes-of-ruptured-uterus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Prenatally, a ruptured uterus may occur due to a weak scar. During labour and delivery or when not in labour a ruptured uterus may occur as a result of:</p>
<ul>
<li>Obstructed labour, for example in malpresentation, cephalopelvic disproportion, contracted pelvis</li>
<li>Excessive or injudicious use of oxytocin</li>
<li>Intrauterine manipulation, for example, internal cephalic version of second twin</li>
<li>Forceps delivery and vacuum extraction</li>
<li>Shirodkar stitch in labour</li>
<li>Rigid cervix</li>
<li>Breech delivery</li>
<li>Multiparity, due to the degeneration of the uterine muscle</li>
<li>Previous scar</li>
<li>Manual removal of placenta</li>
<li>Perforation of uterus</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>During labour a classical scar is more likely to rupture than the lower segment scar.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="early-signs-of-scar-rupture">Early Signs of Scar Rupture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#early-signs-of-scar-rupture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Early signs of scar rupture include a constant lower abnormal pain. This pain worsens during a contraction. There is fresh bleeding, which may be mistaken for show. Contractions may continue but the cervical os fails to dilate. Pulse rate is raised due to shock and tends to increase slowly.</p>
<p>Vigilant observation is required for a mother with a uterine scar showing the above signs so that she can be sectioned before rupture occurs.</p>
<p>Epidural analgesia masks the early signs, and is therefore contraindicated in the mother with a caesarean scar. In the advanced stage, the mother complains of severe and drastic pain, which is continuous and does not correspond to the uterine action. When the scar rupture contraction ceases, the mother rapidly becomes shocked. Rupture through a scar has less chance of infection than a rupture due to obstructed labour.</p>
<p>The presenting part does not descend to the pelvic brim in spite of strong contraction. The cervical os dilates slowly and hangs loosely like an empty sleeve and the membranes rupture early or the bag of water is elongated like a sausage.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-late-signs-of-scar-rupture">The Late Signs of Scar Rupture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-late-signs-of-scar-rupture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following are some of the late signs associated with scar rupture:</p>
<ul>
<li>Mother is dehydrated, shows ketosis and is in severe pain</li>
<li>Rapid pulse and pyrexia of over 38Â°C</li>
<li>Poor urinary output, concentrated with ketosis and often blood stained</li>
<li>Uterus gets moulded round the foetus</li>
<li>Strong uterine contraction, which does not relax between contractions</li>
<li>A Bundle's ring</li>
<li>On vaginal examination, the vagina is hot and dry</li>
<li>Presenting part is high, wedged and immovable</li>
<li>There is over lapping of foetal bones and big caput succedaneum</li>
<li>The mother is exhausted before the rupture, and she will probably cry out during the rupture and complain of a sharp pain in the lower abdomen</li>
<li>She feels something has given way and soon presents with shock</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rupture-secondary-to-manipulation">Rupture Secondary to Manipulation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rupture-secondary-to-manipulation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The general condition of the mother will change, and this could be discovered when the hand is still in the uterus. After any difficult manipulation, the uterus must be explored to rule out injury or rupture. Caesarean section is preferred to difficult manipulation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rupture-secondary-to-oxytocic-drugs">Rupture Secondary to Oxytocic Drugs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rupture-secondary-to-oxytocic-drugs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is common when close monitoring is not done. There is less danger when these drugs are used as a dilute in an intravenous drip. The risk is much greater in multipara where many cases of rupture have followed unmonitored use of oxytocic drugs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="write-down-all-that-you-can-think-of-in-the-management-of-ruptured-uterus">Write down all that you can think of in the management of ruptured uterus.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#write-down-all-that-you-can-think-of-in-the-management-of-ruptured-uterus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The management of a ruptured uterus</p>
<ul>
<li>Combat shock by putting up an intravenous drip of saline and elevate foot of the bed</li>
<li>Inform the doctor or theatre staff to prepare for an emergency caesarean section</li>
<li>Prepare the mother physically and psychologically for theatre</li>
<li>Take blood for grouping and cross matching</li>
</ul>
<p>The options to perform a hysterectomy or to repair the rupture depend on the extent of the trauma and the mother's condition. A hysterectomy is done if the rupture is beyond repair. If the rupture is repaired, you should stress to the mother the importance of not conceiving until at least a year after the operation. Emphasise that she should always deliver by caesarean section and there should be no trial of labour whatsoever.</p>
<p>Post operative care should be followed as in the case of caesarean section.</p>
<p>Can you think of all the complications that may occur?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="there-are-several-complications-of-ruptured-uterus-to-the-mother-these-include">There are several complications of ruptured uterus to the mother. These include:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#there-are-several-complications-of-ruptured-uterus-to-the-mother-these-include" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Paralytic ileus</li>
<li>Peritonitis</li>
<li>Septicaemia</li>
<li>Urinary tract infection</li>
<li>Renal failure</li>
<li>Death</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>The foetus may experience complications such as birth asphyxia, stillbirths in complete rupture and neonatal death.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention-of-uterus-ruptur">Prevention of Uterus Ruptur<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-of-uterus-ruptur" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Prevention is possible through good antenatal care after a thorough history taking. Refer high risk patients with previous scars and contracted pelvis for assessment. Vigilant observations in labour, especially in trial and induction of labour are necessary. You should be able to recognise, at an early stage, signs of obstructed labour and ruptured uterus. Maternal education is important in case of risk factors such as a previous scar. The community should be educated on pregnancy and childbirth complications. They should be advised on the need to deliver in a hospital rather than at home.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="shoulder-dystocia">Shoulder Dystocia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#shoulder-dystocia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Shoulder dystocia is said to have occurred when there is:</p>
<ul>
<li>Failure of the shoulder to rotate spontaneously into anterior, posterior diameter of the pelvis outlet after delivery of the head</li>
</ul>
<p>Predisposing factors include unusually large infants, foetal anomalies, post term pregnancy, maternal age of over 35 years and high parity. You should watch out for the following warning signs:</p>
<ul>
<li>There is slow advance of the head and failure of the head to rotate externally following restitution</li>
<li>Slow crowning of the head</li>
<li>There are difficulties in extension of the face during delivery of the head</li>
<li>There is slow restitution of the occiput to the lateral position</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-shoulder-dystocia">Management of Shoulder Dystocia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-shoulder-dystocia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You should take the following steps as part of the process of management:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Explain the situation to the mother and reassure her.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Position the mother either left lateral with buttocks at the edge of the bed or lithotomy with buttocks slightly beyond the edge.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ask your assistant to summon the doctor.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If the shoulder is caught on the pubic bone the rotation will fail.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Your assistant should try to dislodge the shoulder. They push the anterior shoulder abdominally towards the middle line, while at the same time you attempt to pull the same shoulder forwards vaginally.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Simultaneously apply traction to the head on downward, backward direction.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>When you have managed to bring down the anterior shoulder, rotate and deliver it.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="change-of-the-maternal-position-to-help-release-the-foetal-shoulders">Change of the Maternal Position to Help Release the Foetal Shoulders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#change-of-the-maternal-position-to-help-release-the-foetal-shoulders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>McRober's manoeuvre is a useful position. The mother is asked and helped to lie flat on her back and bring her knees as far as possible up to the chest. This manoeuvre has been proved to rotate the symphysis pubic angle posteriorly. The midwife creates pressure gently at the mother's legs and her abdomen. The impact of the anterior shoulder is released by this pressure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="supra-pubic-pressure">Supra Pubic Pressure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#supra-pubic-pressure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Make an episiotomy to enlarge the outlet and reduce pressure at the pelvic floor.</p>
<p>While you exert traction to the head downwards and backwards, hook the fingers into anterior axilla and rotate its shoulder forwards. When the shoulders are in anterior posterior, conduct the delivery as usual.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rubin-s-manoeuvre">Rubin's Manoeuvre<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rubin-s-manoeuvre" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Identify the posterior shoulder on vaginal examination. Insert two fingers and push the posterior shoulder in the direction of the foetus chest. If the anterior shoulder dislodges, rotate it away from the symphysis pubic and deliver the anterior shoulder.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="zavanelli-manoeuvre">Zavanelli Manoeuvre<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#zavanelli-manoeuvre" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This manoeuvre is done as a last option to save the life of the baby. The obstetrician reinserts the head into the vagina by reversing the mechanism and caesarean section is done immediately.</p>
<p>Advise the mother on the proper diet to prevent big babies. A proper assessment of the baby's size at 36 weeks by the doctor should enable you to decide the proper mode of delivery.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>There are several complications associated with shoulder dystocia.</p>
<ul>
<li>Two thirds of the patients will have blood loss of more than 1,000mls</li>
<li>Maternal death may result from uterine rupture</li>
<li>Neonatal asphyxia</li>
<li>Brachial plexus injury</li>
<li>Neonatal morbidity may be as high as 42%</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="amniotic-fluid-embolism">Amniotic Fluid Embolism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#amniotic-fluid-embolism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a very rare catastrophic condition. Amniotic fluid embolism can occur at any gestation but it is most common at the end of the first stage of labour. The amniotic fluid enters the maternal circulation through the uteri-placental site. An emboli is formed which obstructs one of the pulmonary arteries or alveolar capillaries. It is associated with strong contractions, the membranes having ruptured. The body responds into two phases.</p>
<p>In the initial phase, the pulmonary artery goes into vaso spasm causing hypoxia. In the second phase, there is left ventricular failure, haemorrhage, and blood coagulation disorders followed by pulmonary oedema.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="can-you-think-of-what-the-predisposing-factor-and-signs-and-symptoms-are">Can you think of what the predisposing factor and signs and symptoms are?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#can-you-think-of-what-the-predisposing-factor-and-signs-and-symptoms-are" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="predisposing-factors-include">Predisposing factors include:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#predisposing-factors-include" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Hypertonic uterine action</li>
<li>Placenta abruptio, where the barrier between maternal circulation and amniotic sack is breached and the placenta bed is disrupted</li>
<li>Procedures like insertion of intrauterine catheter</li>
<li>Rupture of membranes</li>
<li>Caesarean section</li>
<li>Inter-uterine manipulation, for example, podalic version</li>
</ul>
<p>Signs and symptoms of the condition are as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Onset of sudden maternal respiratory distress</li>
<li>Severe dyspnoea</li>
<li>Cyanosis</li>
<li>Hypotension</li>
</ul>
<p>The mother may get convulsions and collapse immediately after.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-amniotic-fluid-embolism">Management of Amniotic Fluid Embolism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-amniotic-fluid-embolism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following procedures should be followed when trying to manage the condition:</p>
<ul>
<li>Administer oxygen</li>
<li>Commence resuscitation at once</li>
<li>Give aminophyllin slowly to reduce bronchial spasm</li>
<li>Give fresh blood or fibrinogen to combat hypofibrinogen anaemia</li>
<li>Maintain an intake and output chart checking on urinary output</li>
<li>Assist delivery</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="in-most-cases-the-prognosis-is-poor">In most cases the prognosis is poor.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#in-most-cases-the-prognosis-is-poor" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The mother should be transferred to the intensive care unit.</p>
<p>You should always remember that there are several complications associated with the condition and you should be on the look out for them. These include disseminated intravascular coagulation, haemorrhage before amniotic fluid embolism and acute renal failure. Also note that perinatal mortality and morbidity are high if amniotic fluid embolism occurs before the birth of the baby.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="acute-inversion-of-the-uterus">Acute Inversion of the Uterus<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acute-inversion-of-the-uterus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Inversion of the uterus refers to when the uterus has turned inside out.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classify-the-three-degrees-of-inversion-of-the-uterus">Classify the three degrees of inversion of the uterus.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classify-the-three-degrees-of-inversion-of-the-uterus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="inversion-can-be-classified-as-follows">Inversion can be classified as follows:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#inversion-can-be-classified-as-follows" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>First degree, where the fundus reaches the internal os.</li>
<li>Second degree, where the corpus of the uterus is inverted to internal os.</li>
<li>Third degree, where both the uterus, cervix and vagina are inverted and are visible at the vagina.</li>
</ul>
<p>Classification of inversions is also based on the time they occur. Acute refers to immediate prolapsed after delivery while the placenta is still attached. SubcutÐµ and chronic refer to an inversion, which happens 24 hours later.</p>
<p>Classification of inversions is also based on the time they occur. Acute refers to immediate prolapsed after delivery while the placenta is still attached. SubcutÐµ and chronic refer to an inversion, which happens 24 hours later.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>The predisposing causes of acute inversion of the uterus:</p>
<ul>
<li>Management of third stage of labour</li>
<li>Combining fundal pressure and cord traction while conducting third stage</li>
<li>Use of fundal pressure before separation of placenta</li>
<li>Placenta acreta</li>
<li>Unknown cause, such as when the prolapse happens spontaneously</li>
<li>Sudden emptying of the gravid uterus</li>
<li>Short cord</li>
</ul>
<p>How can you diagnose acute inversion of the uterus?</p>
<p>How to diagnose acute inversion of the uterus:</p>
<ul>
<li>Haemorrhage between 800ml â€“ 1,880ml, which depends on the degree of placenta adherent on the uterine wall</li>
<li>Shock due to pain, which is caused by the stretching of peritoneal nerves and the ovaries being pulled</li>
<li>No fundus is palpable abdominally</li>
<li>If inversion is partial, the fundus will not be visible per vagina</li>
<li>On vaginal examination a mass may be felt</li>
</ul>
<p>The faster the inversion is reversed, the less the risk to the mother. Remember that in an emergency you need effective teamwork. The more medical personnel, the quicker the work is done.</p>
<p>Management of Uterus Inversion</p>
<p>Inform the mother what has happened and reassure her. Call for help from other midwives.</p>
<p>Give instructions as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>One of your assistants should call the doctor</li>
<li>The other assistant should elevate the foot of the bed and give pethidine 100gm to relieve pain</li>
<li>The assistant should then fix a canular, remove blood for cross match and fix an infusion</li>
</ul>
<p>You should then use Johnson's manoeuvre. Try to push the fundus using the palm of your hand. Direct the fundus to the vagina and towards the posterior fornix. Lift the uterus towards the membranes with steady pressure and return to position. Once the uterus is in position, instruct your assistant to give 0.5gm oxitocin while you hold the uterus until you feel a hard contraction. Then the placenta can be delivered by using control cord traction. If the doctor comes before you finish the procedure, they can take over. In a health centre you should refer the mother to the hospital if you succeed, for further management.</p>
<p>Insert a giving set into the vagina. Use one hand to seal the vaginal orifice. Your assistant should instil a warm saline through the giving set. After pouring several litres the pressure will build up in the vagina. This pressure will restore the normal position of the uterus.</p>
<p>Medical Management of Inverted Uterus</p>
<p>If inversion is not possible manually, it may be due to a cervical constriction ring. The doctor will prescribe a relaxant to relax the cervical os and facilitate the replacement of the inversion.</p>
<p>Hypovalaemic Shock</p>
<p>You have come across haemorrhaging in the previous topic. Haemorrhaging is one of the main contributors to shock. You will now look at how bleeding causes shock.</p>
<p>Shock</p>
<p>Shock refers to the collapse of the circulation system, which results in the reduction of blood flow to the tissue. This causes dysfunction of organs and cells. In obstetric shock, the condition may be due to complications of pregnancy and labour.</p>
<p>Shock can be divided into three categories; can you think what these are?</p>
<p>Three types of shock</p>
<ul>
<li>Hypovolaemic, which is as a result of reduction in intravascular volume.</li>
<li>Cardiogenic, which is due to the inability of the heart to pump blood.</li>
<li>Distribute, which results from a malfunction in the vascular system causing maldistribution of the circulatory systems. This can be caused by septic and anaphylactic shock.</li>
</ul>
<p>There are several causes of obstetric shock.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 210 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-causes-of-obstetric-shock">The causes of obstetric shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-causes-of-obstetric-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Haemorrhage during pregnancy, labour and puerperium</li>
<li>Obstetric trauma such as difficult instrumental delivery, forcible breech extraction, manual removal of placenta or caesarean section</li>
<li>Prolonged labour</li>
<li>Fluid loss, for instance, excessive diuresis or hyperemesis gravidarum</li>
<li>Supine hypotensive syndrome</li>
<li>Pulmonary embolism, which may dislodge and cause oxygen deprivation</li>
<li>Reaction due to blood transfusion or drugs</li>
</ul>
<p>The condition develops in several stages, which you will now study in detail.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-initial-stage">The Initial Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-initial-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The venous return to the heart is decreased due to reduction of blood or fluid. There is also a reduction in stroke volume and cardiac output caused by inadequately filled ventricles of the heart. There is a fall in blood pressure, which decreases oxygen supply to the tissue and affects the function of the cells.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="compensatory-stage">Compensatory Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#compensatory-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>At this stage the body can compensate for up to 10% of fluid volume. When it reaches 20-28% it begins to fall. The sympathetic nervous system responds to a drop in cardiac output by constricting vessels in the gastro-intestinal tract, kidney, skin and lungs. This causes the skin to be pale and cool. Peristalsis slows, urinary output is poor and gas exchange in the lungs is impaired. This causes ischaemia and collapse of alveoli, ultimately leading to respiratory failure known as 'adult respiratory distress syndrome'. Blood is distributed to the vital organs only.</p>
<p>There is an increased heart rate in an effort to improve cardiac output and blood pressure. The pupils dilate and the sweat glands are stimulated causing the skin to be moist and clammy. Adrenaline and aldosterone from the adrenal medulla and adrenal cortex are released. The posterior pituitary lobe also produces an antidiuretic hormone, which causes vasoconstriction in an attempt to improve cardiac output. Venous return to the heart increases but cannot be sustained for long unless fluid loss is replaced.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="looking-at-this-mechanism-you-will-notice-how-important-it-is-to-replace-the-lost-fluid-hypovolaemic-stage">Looking at this mechanism you will notice how important it is to replace the lost fluid. Hypovolaemic Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#looking-at-this-mechanism-you-will-notice-how-important-it-is-to-replace-the-lost-fluid-hypovolaemic-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When the compensatory mechanism begins to fail, there is a further fall in cardiac output and blood pressure. Vital organs lack perfusion and coronary arteries lack supply. There is poor peripheral circulation. The pulse is either weak or absent. In the brain, the level of consciousness deteriorates and the mother becomes increasingly unresponsive.</p>
<p>The renal tubules become ischaemic, leading to kidney failure. Waste products such as urea and creatinine are not excreted, resulting in their increased presence in the blood. The gut's function as barrier fails due to ischaemia and gram-negative bacteria are able to enter the blood stream.</p>
<p>The liver can no longer metabolise drugs and hormones. As the bilirubin can no longer be conjugated, it builds up and jaundice develops. With the liver failing to act as a filter, there is a build up of lactic acid and ammonia in the blood due to the failure of waste metabolism. Liver enzymes are released in the blood circulation by dead hepatic cells.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="final-irreversible-stage-of-shock">Final Irreversible Stage of Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#final-irreversible-stage-of-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The distractions of the cells are irreparable causing multisystem failure and ultimately death.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="outcome-of-shock">Outcome of Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#outcome-of-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Early arrest of the cause and replacement of body fluid will give full recovery.</p>
<p>At times the mother may survive but develop permanent damage to various organs. This is referred to as Sheehan's syndrome. Death is usually due to a delay in treatment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-hypovolaemic-shock">Management of Hypovolaemic Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-hypovolaemic-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Urgent resuscitation measures should be applied to prevent irreversible damage to the patient.</p>
<p>The first thing you should do is to maintain a clear airway by turning her on one side. If she is unconscious, insert an airway by turning her on one side and administer oxygen, 40% at the rate of four to six litres.</p>
<p>Find the source of bleeding, whenever possible and try to stop the bleeding. Replace fluid immediately. Take blood for a cross match and give blood transfusion as and when ready. Meanwhile a plasma expander such as dextran,</p>
<p>haemocel or glucose saline (1 litre) should be administered as soon as possible.</p>
<p>When the blood is ready, the first 1,200mls should be given rapidly (within 30 minutes). The doctor should remain with the patient during this exercise. Avoid excessive warmth as it will interfere with the constriction of the peripheral blood vessels, which usually occurs in response to shock.</p>
<p><strong>Remember:</strong>
<strong>Constriction of blood vessels improves cardiac output and blood pressure.</strong></p>
<p>Elevate the foot of the bed by 30cm. This will raise blood pressure 10mm Hg by gravity. This allows the blood to flow to vital centres in the brain.</p>
<p>Hydrocortisone 100-500mg is given slowly in cases of suprarenal failure. A sedative may be necessary in the case of restlessness to calm an apprehensive patient.</p>
<p><strong>Observations</strong></p>
<p>The following observations should be made while monitoring the patient:</p>
<ul>
<li>Assess level of consciousness, noting signs of restlessness or confusion</li>
<li>Monitor blood pressure continuously, about every 15-30 minutes</li>
<li>Cardiac rhythm needs to be monitored continuously</li>
<li>Measure urine output hourly by the use of indwelling catheter</li>
<li>Take hourly temperature and observe the colour of the skin. Improvement to normal body temperature and colour may mean the function of the organs is going back to normal. Persistence of subnormal temperature means the reverse</li>
<li>The infusion quantity and rate should be maintained accurately by measuring haemodynamic pressure in the right atrium</li>
<li>Observe further occurrence of bleeding</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Septic Shock</strong></p>
<p>This is also known as endotoxic or bactereamic shock. The main cause of septic shock is gram-negative organism such as Escherichia coli, Bacillus Proteus or Pseudomonas pyocyaneus.</p>
<p>These organisms are commonly pathogenic in the female genital tract. Gram-positive bacteria, viral or fungal infection, do not commonly cause septic shock. In 20-30% of cases, the cause of infection is combined organisms, and treatment becomes complex.</p>
<p><strong>Can you think what the causes of septic shock are?</strong></p>
<p><strong>Septic Shock</strong></p>
<p>Septic shock can be caused by prolonged rupture of membranes and puerperal sepsis, especially in cases of caesarean section.</p>
<p>The primary response of the body to septic shock is the release of histamine and enzymes produced by damaged cells. This contributes to an increase in the permeability of capillaries and vasodilation. Mediators of opposite action are also produced causing vasoconstriction. Vasodilation is the overall response and causes the reduction of the systemic vascular resistance. Cardiac output remains elevated during this first phase of vasodilation. The mother's face is flushed, her skin is warm and moist. Temperature ranges between 38 and 41Â°C. Systolic blood pressure is less than 90mm Hg.</p>
<p>In the second or late phase of vasoconstriction, there is cold hypotension. The continuity of hypotension and vasodilation causes damage to the kidneys with reduced glomerular filtration due to the constriction of lobules and acute tubular necrosis followed by oliguria.</p>
<p>Adult respiratory syndrome is present in many cases. Haemorrhage occurs due to disseminated intravascular depression, which results in multisystem organ failure. There is mental confusion, coma and cardiac failure, which result in death.</p>
<p>Identify the source of infection by taking specimens including high vaginal swab, multistream urine and blood cultures. Check the infusion site and indwelling catheter for signs of contamination and change where appropriate.</p>
<p><strong>Drug Therapy for Septic Shock</strong></p>
<p>Use quick fluid therapy including glucose, saline, rigers, lactate or whole. An injection of dopamine, 20mg per kilogram, is infused in the vasodilation stage. Hydrocortisone is given, 100mgs IV stat, followed by 100mg six hourly until the pulse and blood pressure are stabilised.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Antibiotics are commenced immediately after the specimens for culture and other investigations are completed. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Gentamycin 80mg IV eight hourly</li>
<li>Metronidazole 500mg IV eight hourly</li>
<li>Ampicillin 500mg IV six hourly</li>
<li>These should be administered until the bowel sound returns. You should then continue with 400mg metronidazole orally eight hourly for 10 days.</li>
</ul>
